From prem at songbirdtech.com  Fri May 25 16:34:33 2007
From: prem at songbirdtech.com (Prem Kurian Philip)
Date: Fri May 25 16:34:47 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Five crucial things the Linux community doesn't
 understand about the average computer user 
Message-ID: <49689.61.247.255.57.1180091073.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>

The answer to the question - why doesn't Linux gain more market share
considering all the problems with Windows..

Manoj Srivastava raises some interesting points which highlights exactly
the reason why Linux doesn't capture more market share.

I have grouped together related statements to make it easier to respond.

Manoj Srivastava wrote:

>> I think one of the things that the linux community does not understand
>> about ordinary users is that:
>
>         I think that people who write things like this have no clue
>  about what drives the Linux community.  The lack of understanding of
>  what drives free software developers
>

Also:

>         Feeding hangers on who just want a free meal is not really a
>  primary motivation.  Sure, having the meal in the open where anyone can
>  pick up a fork lets new fork-weilders join in, but making extraordinary
>  effort to feed the non-participating is not something very likely to
>  happen on a broad scale.


>   2) Free software is about community; and people who care and
>      participate, it is not a marketing machine; it is a group of people
>      getting together to help each other achieve more from computers.
>
>         These people are not part of the free software community, and I
>  see no real reason to try to cater to them.  Free software is about
>  community; is it surprising that no extraordinary effort is made to
>  pander to those who are resistant to inclusion?
>
>         Again, this is not about gaining market share.

>    5) Free software is written by people who have an itch to scratch,
>       and for like minded people. The basis is what I label as "The
>       Dinid Philosophers Solution" -- it is written for other people who
>       pick up the fork and help feed each other.
>

Understood. That is a valid point. Linux developers create software for
themselves not for others and if others too benefit from the software
which the linux users created for themselves, then it is an added bonus,
but if not, nothing is lost for the linux developer. I agree with you on
that assessment.

Perhaps ordinary users have very different types of needs than does a
Linux developer, which is perhaps why they don't use Linux as much.

And there is nothing wrong in that. It is fair that a Linux developer
develops it for his own benefit and not for others.

If the consensus among Linux developers is that Linux is developed only
for themselves and others with the exact same needs (other geeks), then we
don't need to continue this discussion further about why regular users are
not flocking to linux.

On difficulty with installing hardware on Linux:
>
>   1) A computer is an expensive, useful, general purpose
>      tool. Researching the purchase of the machine and peripherals for
>      compatibility seems reasonable.  As long as it is possible to
>      accomplish the task with some hardware, it does not matter that
>      some hardware vendors make incompatible equipment.
>
>         Actually, I think it is easier than getting  things not blessed
>  by the Vendor to work on Vista.  And now that some hardware
>  manufacturers are pre-installing Linux, it means that drivers are often
>  available (Dell distributes their printer driver for my color laser as
>  a GPL'd .deb).
>
>
>         Frankly, I would not buy hardware before learning whether it
>  supports Linux; and boycott the other hardware vendors.
>

IMHO, interesting but ultimately pointless exercise because Linux does not
yet have the kind of numbers to interest hardware makers.

It comes back to your point - perhaps the hardware makers have decided to
target only Windows and Apple users. And that is fair too.

>
>> 2) For most people, OS is not a religion. Most users won't even know
>>    what is an OS and neither do they care to know.
>
>> 3) You get very little support if you call up BSNL/Airtel etc and ask
>>    why your DSL modem is not working with the Linux distro installed
>>    on your  machine.
>   3) The free software community helps each other; and the network has
>      the results of people sharing how they made things work, and the
>      pitfalls to avoid.
>
>         ISP's, retail shop salespeople, hardware vendors might not be
>  clued in to free software, which is only recently encroaching into the
>  mainstream, but the community provides its own resources to each
>  other. This is why this LUG is here, and why people participate.
>

Yes, but not all users hang out on ilug. And expecting them to hang out on
an internet user group so that they can just get their internet connection
working is perhaps not the right approach to solving the problem.


Regards,
PK

P.S: Apologies for posting the entire message thread the last time around.
I hit the send button by mistake on that before I cut out all the other
messages and before I had entered my reply.
From anant at kix.in  Fri May 25 16:47:20 2007
From: anant at kix.in (Anant Narayanan)

Subject: [Ilugc] Five crucial things outsiders do not understand about
	the	Linux community
In-Reply-To: <87ps4qtgs5.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>
References: <55666.61.247.255.57.1179920205.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>
	<87ps4qtgs5.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>
Message-ID: <4656C5C0.80402@kix.in>

Manoj Srivastava wrote:
>    5) Free software is written by people who have an itch to scratch,
>       and for like minded people. The basis is what I label as "The
>       Dinid Philosophers Solution" -- it is written for other people who
>       pick up the fork and help feed each other.

The problem for several of us is at a more lower, social, level. The
issue here is not just about us helping each other to utilize our
computers in a better manner but also about eliminating the grip that
the mammoth companies have over our society.

Free software is also about educating "normal people" about the evils of
proprietary software. If we don't try and tell other people as to why
free software is better than proprietary software, then evils such as
DRM, patents on software & algorithms, and proprietary software itself
cannot be eliminated.

Free software, Free world. That's encompasses a lot more than just
philosophers feeding each other.

Cheers,

From anoopjohn at gmail.com  Fri May 25 18:31:36 2007
From: anoopjohn at gmail.com (Anoop John)
Date: Fri May 25 18:31:43 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Printing from web applications
Message-ID: <406f1f3f0705250601v1e821015r29cfbf70ceef6dbb@mail.gmail.com>

One difficulty that linux faces in govt enterprises is the profusion
of vb and access based applications in govt departments which makes
transition from windows to linux impossible. You can get vb+access
programmers at dime a dozen and the development time is also very
minimal. A competing technology would be php+mysql applications. PHP
based web applications have a comparable development time when
compared with VB applications. Additionally web based applications
would make the transitioning from windows to linux much easier because
the application would be run from the browser in both cases. But the
catch is that you dont have the level of control over printing from
web applications that you have from a vb application. Yes you have
some css based control in terms of margins, padding etc. But when it
comes to creating content suited to page sizes it is very difficult.
Another issue is pagination. When you have content spanning multiple
pages you barely have any control in terms of the positions of the
content. You have some control in terms of page breaks but that too
very laboriously.
So is there some simple standard and easily reproducible solution to
this problem. If there is, then it would be the key to large scale
migrations from windows to linux machines in govt establishments.
Cheers
Anoop John
www.zyxware.com

From anant at kix.in  Fri May 25 19:28:14 2007
From: anant at kix.in (Anant Narayanan)
Date: Fri May 25 19:28:41 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Printing from web applications
In-Reply-To: <406f1f3f0705250601v1e821015r29cfbf70ceef6dbb@mail.gmail.com>
References: <406f1f3f0705250601v1e821015r29cfbf70ceef6dbb@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4656EB76.801@kix.in>

Anoop John wrote:
> the application would be run from the browser in both cases. But the
> catch is that you dont have the level of control over printing from
> web applications that you have from a vb application. Yes you have

Huh? Why would a *server-side* script have control over the client's
printer? If you need printing on a local machine, feel free to write a
PHP-CLI application that does the same.

Alternatively, you can write PHP that generated JavaScript to do the
printing, just like what happens when a user clicks on the 'print this
page' button on several websites.

> some css based control in terms of margins, padding etc. But when it
> comes to creating content suited to page sizes it is very difficult.
> Another issue is pagination. When you have content spanning multiple
> pages you barely have any control in terms of the positions of the
> content. You have some control in terms of page breaks but that too
> very laboriously.

Untrue. Pagination with PHP works just fine. Do a Google search for
'pagination with PHP' and see how many scripts that turns up. Maybe it's
not as easy as VB's 'drag-and-drop', but do companies pay programmers
just to drag-and-drop stuff all day long?

> So is there some simple standard and easily reproducible solution to
> this problem. If there is, then it would be the key to large scale
> migrations from windows to linux machines in govt establishments.

The fact is that you can achieve the same results (if not better) with
open source solutions, atleast in the web arena. The
companies/government et al. are just not aware of it. Even that scenario
is changing given the sudden shift of emphasis of the industry to PHP.
Look at the number of jobs being offered to PHP programmers, there's
more demand than supply in India.

-- 
Anant Narayanan
http://www.kix.in/
gpg --recv-keys 0EF6914E
<anant@kix.in,gentoo.org,php.net>
From srivasta at debian.org  Fri May 25 19:47:25 2007

On Fri, 25 May 2007 16:47:20 +0530, Anant Narayanan
<anant@kix.in> said:  

> The problem for several of us is at a more lower, social, level. The
> issue here is not just about us helping each other to utilize our
> computers in a better manner but also about eliminating the grip that
> the mammoth companies have over our society.

> Free software is also about educating "normal people" about the evils
> of proprietary software. If we don't try and tell other people as to
> why free software is better than proprietary software, then evils such
> as DRM, patents on software & algorithms, and proprietary software
> itself cannot be eliminated.

> Free software, Free world. That's encompasses a lot more than just
> philosophers feeding each other.

        This is not an unlaudable goal.  I  wonder, though, how
 prevalent that view is amongst the worker bees of free software, as
 opposed to the armchair intellectuals.  In my experience with people in
 free software development, I have never heard the goal above stated as
 a major motivator (and never heard it stated so succinctly).

        The original discussion revolved around what needs to be done by
 the creators of free software -- and while saving the world from
 software patents and DRM can't be argued against, it usually lies in
 the same sphere as ending world hunger or curing aids. Nice, but rarely
 does one spend 20-40 hours a week doing that in ones free time, unless
 one is very very unusually motivated.

        The result is that the general free software development flows
 down the scratching ones itch route.  If people are truly motivated
 in catering to the unwashed masses in order to save the world, they
 need to gird up their lions and actually pitch in and do the work.

        manoj

Don't tell any big lies today.  Small ones can be just as effective.

> Understood. That is a valid point. Linux developers create software
> for themselves not for others and if others too benefit from the
> software which the linux users created for themselves, then it is an
> added bonus, but if not, nothing is lost for the linux developer. I
> agree with you on that assessment.

> Perhaps ordinary users have very different types of needs than does a
> Linux developer, which is perhaps why they don't use Linux as much.

> And there is nothing wrong in that. It is fair that a Linux developer
> develops it for his own benefit and not for others.

> If the consensus among Linux developers is that Linux is developed
> only for themselves and others with the exact same needs (other
> geeks), then we don't need to continue this discussion further about
> why regular users are not flocking to linux.

        I don't pretend to speak for _all_ the developers, and I don't
 think there is a consensus. But I can attest that a sizeable segment of
 the developer community does indeed share my views. The result is that
 the general free software development flows down the scratching ones
 itch route.  If people are truly motivated in catering to the unwashed
 masses in order to save the world, they need to gird up their lions and
 actually pitch in and do the work.

> On difficulty with installing hardware on Linux:
>> 

> IMHO, interesting but ultimately pointless exercise because Linux does
> not yet have the kind of numbers to interest hardware makers.

        I think we are on the cusp of change on that. Dell is now
 preloading Ubuntu, IBM says all their hardware  is compatible with
 Linux; HP also sells Linux on some hardware.

> It comes back to your point - perhaps the hardware makers have decided
> to target only Windows and Apple users. And that is fair too.

        I think you'll see more interest from hardware vendors in the
 future. Heck, Dell has a Debian package for their printer driver.

> Yes, but not all users hang out on ilug. And expecting them to hang
> out on an internet user group so that they can just get their internet
> connection working is perhaps not the right approach to solving the
> problem.

        There are even more venues out on the 'net. Debian user mailing
 list archives are indexed by google.

From anant at kix.in  Fri May 25 20:31:27 2007
From: anant at kix.in (Anant Narayanan)
Date: Fri May 25 20:31:56 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Five crucial things outsiders do not understand about
	the	Linux community
In-Reply-To: <87wsyxrnre.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>
References: <55666.61.247.255.57.1179920205.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>	<87ps4qtgs5.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>	<4656C5C0.80402@kix.in>
	<87wsyxrnre.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>
Message-ID: <4656FA47.5080402@kix.in>

Manoj Srivastava wrote:
>         This is not an unlaudable goal.  I  wonder, though, how
>  prevalent that view is amongst the worker bees of free software, as
>  opposed to the armchair intellectuals.  In my experience with people in
>  free software development, I have never heard the goal above stated as
>  a major motivator (and never heard it stated so succinctly).

It is true that most of the *current* free software is a result of
scratching your own itch. But the entire GNU project was founded with
the exact goal of being a replacement for the then proprietary Unix -
and what good is a replacement that isn't used by the masses?

Over the course of time, free software developers; or the worker bees as
you call them, have indeed forgotten the *original* motivation behind
free software. A trend perhaps started by Linus - I doubt if he really
knew the moral and social implications of releasing his kernel under the
GPL. He did it simply because he really didn't care if the source was
visible to the whole world, something that any university student his
age would have done at the time.

Let's not forget that the "armchair intellectuals" of today were the
"worker bees" of the past, albeit, with different motivations.

>         The original discussion revolved around what needs to be done by
>  the creators of free software -- and while saving the world from
>  software patents and DRM can't be argued against, it usually lies in
>  the same sphere as ending world hunger or curing aids. Nice, but rarely
>  does one spend 20-40 hours a week doing that in ones free time, unless
>  one is very very unusually motivated.

Indeed, saving the world from DRM can hardly serve as a motivator for
writing software these days. However, it certainly plays a role in the
marketing of the software that is written. Regardless of why free
software is written, a small portion of the free software community
*must* be devoted to convincing the masses to actually use that software
- not only for the greater good of the society but also for the
developers of the software itself. I quote from Frederick P. Brooke's
"The Mythical Man-Month":

"Why is programming fun? What delights may its practitioner expect as
his reward? <snip>
The pleasure of making things that are useful to others. Deep within, we
want others to use our work and to find it helpful. In this respect the
programming system is not essentially different from a child's first
clay pencil holder `for daddy's office`"

>         The result is that the general free software development flows
>  down the scratching ones itch route.  If people are truly motivated
>  in catering to the unwashed masses in order to save the world, they
>  need to gird up their lions and actually pitch in and do the work.

I don't question the motivation of free software developers. However, I
oppose the view that free software developers shouldn't care about
whether their software is used by the masses or not. As long as there
are people ready to do the pushing voluntarily, the problem is solved.
The worker bees keep developing software for whatever reason - while
others spend time in convincing the masses to use that software. The
free software community is not only made of developers who can code but
also of people who are interested in spreading its message.

Of course, a small percentage of free software developers *can* afford
to keep "free society" as their major motivator: a vast majority of
university professors and students - who have nothing to lose. And of
course, people who are paid to write free software.

The essence here is not to forget the original goal of free software
development - and one of the objectives is to get the masses to use it.
The OLPC project is an exemplary example.

Regards,
-- 
Anant Narayanan
http://www.kix.in/
gpg --recv-keys 0EF6914E
<anant@kix.in,gentoo.org,php.net>

From anant at kix.in  Fri May 25 22:25:44 2007
From: anant at kix.in (Anant Narayanan)
Date: Fri May 25 22:26:11 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Debian Mailing list
In-Reply-To: <b2600ec40705250947uf5c6e5bm1a5e3c1c616770c1@mail.gmail.com>
References: <b2600ec40705250947uf5c6e5bm1a5e3c1c616770c1@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <46571510.9070702@kix.in>

Antano Solar wrote:
> Today i joined the debian bug  mailing list , hoping to take part in
> the bug fixing process.
> 
> Anyway the mails that come from them are in the number of 100+ in last 6
> hours.
> 
> I am just curious , is there any other efficient way of getting to
> look only at the areas I am interested in.

I use procmail to sort my gentoo-bugs. procmail uses perl-like syntax
and is extremely powerful in terms of sorting mail. I discard bugmail
that isn't relevant to me and send the rest of the bugs into categorized
folders. Since all of the bugmail is generated by bugzilla, it should be
easy to filter.

Thunderbird filters could be made to work like procmail, but aren't as
powerful and not portable since thunderbird works on the client-side
while procmail is permanent server-side organization. However, everyone
doesn't have access to their IMAP/POP server to redirect mail to
procmail - in which case client-side filters are good.

From: krish.arava at gmail.com (krishna chaitanya arava)
Date: Fri May 25 22:37:11 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] bsnl dataone connection in SuSE
In-Reply-To: <a3bcf7260705250055r5d7d1260g697fdbf890933fdc@mail.gmail.com>
References: <a3bcf7260705250055r5d7d1260g697fdbf890933fdc@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <feff2f50705251006q72180104u3df8f4bf098e4c52@mail.gmail.com>

>>the procedure was quite simple for establishing BSNL DATAONE connection on
>>SuSE.
I had some problems in Ubuntu 6.10 an 7.04 like hibernating.
I shifted to Opensuse and it works very well ,
USB network card configuration , Hibernating very well , NTFS write , glossy
look ,
new GNOME menu interface and yast configuration tool.

I worked with Ubuntu Feisty Fawn and Fedora 6 , but I opt to stay with
Opensuse -- its works very well. ( its just my personal opinion )

1)go to YaST
> 2)select network devices
> 3)click on DSL to create a broadband connection
> 4)add a dsl connection
> 5) in PPP mode select PPP over Ethernet
> 6)configure your ethernet card
>              i)select the appropriate one
>              ii)set the gateway as 192.168.1.1
>              iii)you can set your own ip address or let DHCP provide you a
> dynamic ip address .say set  it to 192.168.1.30.
>             iv) you can set dns server ip by yourself if you want .the
> BSNL
> service provide give you these details.or else the pppd will automatically
> search for DNS and set
> 7)you can activate connection at boot time or you can do it manually.
> 8)now proceed to next step by providing name of the service
> provider.setdataone(your choice)
> 9)set the user name and password for your connection
> 10)now select the checkbox for "automatically retrieve DNS" and "set DNS
> automatically" if you havent set the DNS yourself.
> 11)proceed further and your dsl connection has been set.
>
> a confirmation of this setup is that you get an icon on bottom panel
> resempling a white coloured plug left-click on it to dial in and right
> click
> to disconnect.
>
> you can also set the DNS manually by editing /etc/resolv.conf and adding
> lines as "nameserver xxx.yyy.zzz.www"
>
> you can also manually start the connection by typing" ifup dsl0" at the
> prompt in  terminal
>
>
>
> i hope this helps those who are unable to connect to BSNL dataone in SuSE
>
>

From ambuj.mbp at gmail.com  Fri May 25 23:12:19 2007
From: ambuj.mbp at gmail.com (Ambuj Thacker)
Date: Fri May 25 23:12:36 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Softlinks...
Message-ID: <c021e3c90705251042g5ae10c46qe61dfcf00b8e30ff@mail.gmail.com>

Softlinks can be made by using the command ln -sv <File name> <Destination>

!.The size of the soft link is equal to the text size(each character is of
size 1 Byte) of the PATH of the original file.
!!. The permission of the softlink are lrwxrwxrwx,where 'l' indicates soft
link.
!!!.Only root can make a link in / partition.The owner and the group of the
soft link is root.Still the original file can be modified by its owner or
groupmembers.
!/\ Color of the softlink can be changed from DIR_COLORS directory. In
SuseLinux it is in /etc and is common to all users.

aMBUJ

Subject: [Ilugc] Printing from web applications
In-Reply-To: <e70ef6930705250728u26143ef8weeeca958d525b5cd@mail.gmail.com>

Thanks Mr. Srinivasan for the clarification.
Let me give you a simple example - printing on small format printers
or on continuous sheet printing - managing the width and height is
very difficult from a web application.
I was wondering if there is a java based solution that can process the
html in the page and send to the printer directly without depending on
the browser print functionality. Another aspect I missed in the
previous mail is the ease of use. In a VB application you just click
on print and whoosh it prints. But from a browser you click on print
then the print pops up - you set page size, select pages, remove
header footer and finally you print. Even the pdf method has the same
disadvantage in that you click print, pdf opens in popup then you
click on print and then in the print dialog you click on print to
print the page.

I think Anant mistook me for a VB developer/fan. I used to work for a
company developing using M$ technologies. Now I run my own company and
we have linux as the development environment and PHP and MySQL as the
technologies. But the point is - to create a space in the VB+Access
market it is very tough. I can fight if I have the right weapons.
Printing seems to be a chink in the armor right now.

It doesnt make sense for a government organization to pay M$ 6000 Rs
each for each system just because they want to use VB+Access
applications. The sad thing is the companies dont normally look at
such aspects when then sell their VB application. Who cares if govt
has to send Billions to M$ as licensing fees.

Yes there is an alternative approach using wxWidgets but the cost of
development is much higher given the lack of skilled workforce and the
premiums I will have to pay to get them. PHP+MySQL seems do-able with
the workforce available in the market.

Another reason why I would like to push the web based approach is that
- possibly that would be the most scalable approach for applications
that need to be accessed from across the country. Also it would
possibly be the cheapest for the government.

I am sure somebody must have thought of and implemented a solution to
the above problem.

Cheers
Anoop
www.zyxware.com

$ sudo passwd root
<enter your password>
<enter required root password>
<again>

Then use su to become root.

Ok, let me put it this way - what I'm trying to say is writing code for 
fun as a "selfish expression" has nothing to do with free software. Yes, 
it's just a fact that some of the code written for fun will end up being 
foss, but it's not mandatory. What we mean by "free software" here is 
software written for fun (probably not so true anymore) being 
distributed in a certain way (i.e, under different licences like GPL, 
BSD, etc). What's a distribution mechanism got to do with whether the 
software was written for fun or not?

But, the actual point I wanted to make was not whether this particular 
point is in line with AR's philosophy or not (actually, I agreed with 
you) - the point is that if you look at her philosophy holistically, 
it's goes exactly opposite to what Debian & FSF's stated goals are 
(which is why I presume Manoj wouldn't want to promote her ideas :).


V.

From rajanvn at gmail.com  Sat May 26 12:23:07 2007
From: rajanvn at gmail.com (Natarajan V)
Date: Sat May 26 12:23:17 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Real/Helix player bug fixed - Plays Indian music
Message-ID: <e977d4310705252353r281b7a97t632fc79a76c7b86d@mail.gmail.com>

For Music fans using Linux:

Till date the Indian Music sites including Raaga.com, MusicIndiaOnline
etc, did not work on Linux/Firefox combination. There was a
Segmentation fault in Helix/Realplayers.

This has now been fixed and works fine. But the same is not available
in the final release of Helix/Real player. For you to listen to
Raaga.com, please download and install the latest nightly builds from
helixcommunity.org. Please remember to create Symbolic links in the
Firefox plugins folder to get the player working.

Related Bug Information:
https://bugs.helixcommunity.org/show_bug.cgi?id=5134

Fix Information:
http://lists.helixcommunity.org/pipermail/player-dev/2007-May/002866.html

With regards,
Natarajan
From prem at songbirdtech.com  Sat May 26 12:55:05 2007
From: prem at songbirdtech.com (Prem Kurian Philip)
Date: Sat May 26 12:55:14 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Re: Five crucial things outsiders do not understand
 about the Linux community
Message-ID: <51395.61.247.255.57.1180164305.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>

I think one of the primary problems with Linux developers/proponents is
they don't seem to understand that market share is IMPORTANT. Actually it
is critical.

There is the common misconception that we can adopt an elitist approach
towards ordinary users and not have that turn around and bite us.

Some examples of this elitist, exclusiveness:

Towards users:
--------------
a) We will target only geeks ... we are scratching our own itch and we
don't care that the software doesn't work they way you want it to.

b) If you need any help setting up anything, just ask the "community" or
RTFM or go to this discussion thread or that IRC.

c) If you need that functionality, just do it yourself.


Towards H/W Manufacturers:
--------------------------
a) If you don't release the specs, we won't write a driver.

b) We will boycott your hardware etc.


I will explain why all these statements and attitudes while fair are
really hurting Linux.

First a reply:

Manoj Srivastava wrote:

>         This is not an unlaudable goal.  I  wonder, though, how
>  prevalent that view is amongst the worker bees of free software, as
>  opposed to the armchair intellectuals.  In my experience with people in
>  free software development, I have never heard the goal above stated as
>  a major motivator (and never heard it stated so succinctly).

The so-called "arm chair intellectuals" that you are referring to are
regular users who may not have programming skills, but may have skills in
other areas.

NOTE 1: Their lack of programming skills does not in any sense make them
or their viewpoint inferior to that of a programmer.

These regular users may want to see the same features they are used to
having on Windows because, after all, some friend of theirs convinced them
to use Linux instead of Windows as Linux is better (and also free) and has
equivalent software.

Having said that I do understand your point of view - you are saying that
a person who does not contribute code towards an open source effort really
doesn't have too much of a voice as the developers are not in any sense
obligated to listen to "just" a ordinary user who is not actively
contributing towards the project.


>         The original discussion revolved around what needs to be done by
>  the creators of free software -- and while saving the world from
>  software patents and DRM can't be argued against, it usually lies in
>  the same sphere as ending world hunger or curing aids. Nice, but rarely
>  does one spend 20-40 hours a week doing that in ones free time, unless
>  one is very very unusually motivated.
>
>         The result is that the general free software development flows
>  down the scratching ones itch route.  If people are truly motivated
>  in catering to the unwashed masses in order to save the world, they
>  need to gird up their lions and actually pitch in and do the work.

I get that as well.

Now the other side of the coin is this - as long as ordinary users don't
throng towards Linux - that is, unless the number of Linux becomes pretty
large (in comparison to MS Windows usage), it is unlikely that Linux
developers/users will have quite the same say as MS / MS Windows
users/developers.

According to the February 2005 edition of Wired, the distribution of users
across OS's was something like this:

Under "Desktop OS":
- Windows: 95%
- Linux: 3%
- Others: 2%

Now, in the past couple of years the number of Linux users may have
increased. Assuming the number of Linux users has now reached 5% (highly
unlikely as that estimate means that the number of Linux users have gone
up by around 80%), Linux users would still feature very low in the radar
for hardware manufacturers.

NOTE 2: It is natural that hardware manufactures want to target the
largest pie in the market (MS Windows) and not Linux.

As much as it is the Linux developer's prerogative to target only geeks,
it is the hardware manufacturers prerogative to target only the biggest
pie in the market - the Windows users. They have the freedom do do that.

NOTE 3: If you want Linux to be taken seriously, we must target the
end-user and build up market share to the point where we can be taken
seriously. There is no way around it.

NOTE 4: If we want to remain a niche group (consisting only of geeks),
that is fine - we have the freedom to do that. But then we cannot decide
that we want the privileges of a mass-market player. We must be willing to
live with the problems of being a niche market player without whining
about the lack of support from hardware manufacturers, software companies,
governments etc.


NOTE 5: On the topic of boycotting hardware manufactures - when the
hardware company decided not to release linux drivers (or even specs) for
their hardware, it was a clear statement from them that they are not
interested in linux users. That is, Linux users are not invited into the
party they are throwing.

Boycotting such a hardware manufacturer is like sending an angry "No, I am
not coming to your party" mail to someone when they haven't invited you to
the party in the first place.

Thoughts?

Regards,
PK

From siva.ilugc at gmail.com  Sat May 26 14:33:51 2007
From: siva.ilugc at gmail.com (Sivasankar Chander)
Date: Sat May 26 14:34:02 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] [OT] Which one is better
In-Reply-To: <1375fd8a0705251756i28be1a42r4236270db7a792da@mail.gmail.com>
References: <69621d830705251004s2db21195h854e67c249850a30@mail.gmail.com>
	<1375fd8a0705251756i28be1a42r4236270db7a792da@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <a8f990120705260203w49197782t92b6a59424e4beb7@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/26/07, mehul <mehul.forums@gmail.com> wrote:
>
>
> Clockspeed isn't the only factor. C2D with 1.86Ghz can leave a 3.8 Ghz
> P4EE
> way behind.
>
> If you can afford, C2D e6320 is best bang for buck processor IMO.
> It runs at 1.86Ghz and has 4mb L2 cache and virtualisation support. And
> costs
> just a couple of hundred more than e6300. Not sure of it's availability
> though.


IMHO, the best bang-for-the-buck presently is the Allandale Core 2 Duo
E4300 at 1.80GHz/800FSB/2MB L2 cache, at ~Rs.5k for the CPU. It
overclocks easily to 3.4 GHz, at which speed it leaves pretty much
everything, including Quad-Core Core 2 Extremes, biting the dust.
The Allandale overclocking is actually limited by the Northbridge
power dissipation - they get too hot when the FSB is ramped up.

  Every few years, there is an exceptional value CPU that emerges from
Intel that blows away everything else in price/performance - past examples
include the Mendocino Celeron 300A, Coppermine Celeron 533A, Tualatin
Celeron 1200, Northwood Mobile Celeron 2400, etc. The present example
is the Allandale 1.80. It's probably roughly equivalent to a P4 at 10 to 15
GHz, which of course will never show up, since the P4 was phased out
in February. Old Stocks of P4 CPUs will continue to be available at
retailers
for several months, though.

-Siva

P.S. There's utterly no point in buying any P4 now, unless you're upgrading
a legacy system. P4s run too hot, and their performance is way too low
on GNU/Linux integer/pointer-chasing code, compared to the alternatives.

From mail-lists at karan.org  Sat May 26 17:56:04 2007
From: mail-lists at karan.org (Karanbir Singh)
Date: Sat May 26 17:55:56 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Solaris in RHEL 5
In-Reply-To: <4656E50E.3000304@gmail.com>
References: <894877.71551.qm@web36715.mail.mud.yahoo.com>	<46569FBB.2090708@karan.org>
	<4656E50E.3000304@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4658275C.6020900@karan.org>

D????? ?h?kr?v?rth? wrote:
>>   
> what is full-virt ? A virtualizing software like xen/vmware/parallels? 
> I did google, it seems to be related to xen.. But dont really understand
> where it stands

briefly:

Full-virt basically means that your machine supports Virtualisation on
the h/w level and Xen can make use of that to run the guest os, natively
- with no patching / customising required. eg. being able to run Windows
under a Xen hosted environment - or in this case Solaris under a linux
hosted Xen environ.

This is different from para-virt, where the guest os needs to have xen
para-virt support built into itself to run as a guest.

lots of docs out there that explain this stuff...

- KB

-- 
Karanbir Singh : http://www.karan.org/ : 2522219@icq

From rajanvn at gmail.com  Sun May 27 08:40:38 2007
From: rajanvn at gmail.com (Natarajan V)
Date: Sun May 27 08:40:49 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Printing from web applications
In-Reply-To: <46581A1D.7050300@locustechnologies.com>
References: <406f1f3f0705250601v1e821015r29cfbf70ceef6dbb@mail.gmail.com>
	<4656EB76.801@kix.in>
	<e70ef6930705250728u26143ef8weeeca958d525b5cd@mail.gmail.com>
	<406f1f3f0705251112k3b1c3bb1od01e14b53eb0b353@mail.gmail.com>
	<46581A1D.7050300@locustechnologies.com>
Message-ID: <e977d4310705262010s65dae4c2ue70f7fa732085c6b@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/26/07, sbl <sbl@locustechnologies.com> wrote:
>
>
> Anoop John wrote:
> > Let me give you a simple example - printing on small format printers
> > or on continuous sheet printing - managing the width and height is
> > very difficult from a web application.

I am not sure if the following will help you, but give it a try.

What we used to do when we have to print a form through the browser,
is as follows.
1. Use different CSS for print and for screen using MEDIA:PRINT and
MEDIA:SCREEN tags in the Style sheet. This way, we can print what is
not displayed on screen and show what is not printed. We can also
force a page break or a repeat header (repeat one part of the screen
in every page) using the stylesheets.

2. Proivide the user with a button which does a document.print (in
JS). The user can optionally print from file->print.

Cheers,
Natarajan

  expecting to order them around.

Googling suggested turning off the Power Management settings in BIOS.
Or try passing acpi=off kernel parameter.

>
> Also tell me, is it true that Parallels Workstation works only with CDs and not DVDs.
> I think it did not boot from my new Solaris 10 DVD.
>
Parallels Workstation's VM Hardware spec says
<Quote>
Up to four IDE devices, that may be either virtual hard drives (from
20 Mb up to 128 Gb each, mapped to image file), or CD/DVD-ROM drives
(mapped to physical drive or to image file), or both hard drives and
CD/DVD-ROM drives;
</Quote>
So I think it should theoretically boot out of your DVD.

Did you check whether your DVD is proper? Did you try booting your PC
with the DVD before trying with Parallels?

Thanks for the tips Natarajan
> What we used to do when we have to print a form through the browser,
> is as follows.
> 1. Use different CSS for print and for screen using MEDIA:PRINT and
> MEDIA:SCREEN tags in the Style sheet. This way, we can print what is
> not displayed on screen and show what is not printed. We can also
> force a page break or a repeat header (repeat one part of the screen
> in every page) using the stylesheets.

But how do you know where to insert the insert-page-break css? you
dont know how big the content is when you print it right? suppose you
insert a page-break-after every 10 rows in a table and you have a huge
9th row. What will happen is there will be a pagebreak after 9th row
because of the size and after the 10th because of the css.
Additionally i have seen problems with the vertical alignment of
content when there is too little content on a page when the orphan is
a TR of a long table

From anandhg02 at yahoo.com  Sun May 27 21:08:27 2007
From: anandhg02 at yahoo.com (Anandh G)
Date: Sun May 27 21:08:35 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Solaris in RHEL 5
In-Reply-To: <4658275C.6020900@karan.org>
Message-ID: <276157.99923.qm@web36711.mail.mud.yahoo.com>

If you any good link for setting up solaris as guest OS in XEN, can you plz share with me. I tried searching, but was not able to get the required document.

Karanbir Singh <mail-lists@karan.org> wrote:briefly:

Full-virt basically means that your machine supports Virtualisation on
the h/w level and Xen can make use of that to run the guest os, natively
- with no patching / customising required. eg. being able to run Windows
under a Xen hosted environment - or in this case Solaris under a linux
hosted Xen environ.

This is different from para-virt, where the guest os needs to have xen
para-virt support built into itself to run as a guest.

lots of docs out there that explain this stuff...

- KB

From shuveb at gmail.com  Mon May 28 10:36:30 2007

Subject: [Ilugc] [OT] Which one is better
In-Reply-To: <369760.32916.qm@web8713.mail.in.yahoo.com>
References: <69621d830705251004s2db21195h854e67c249850a30@mail.gmail.com>
	<369760.32916.qm@web8713.mail.in.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <c24555040705272206r4dfa8ffodf89cb3bd832ea6c@mail.gmail.com>

Hmm..

> Do you really need a 64 bit processor? 32-bit would be suffice.

Interesting what marketting can do. All current Intel processors
including the Celeron D, Pentium 4, Pentium D and C2D *are* 64 bit
processors. It is just that AMD calls its processors 64 bit
explicitly. AMD's technology is called AMD64, while Intel calls it
Intel 64 bit Architecture (earlier called EMT64). These CPUs are fully
32-bit compatible. So, finding a 32 bit processor in the market is
actually more difficult, you may actually buy something that is 64 bit
accidentally!

Use this URL to see what features your CPU supports:
http://compare.intel.com

If you got a recent P4, just pop in a 64 bit distro live/install CD
and it should just work. Just that you didn't know it was 64 bit.

When I tried using Jasper reports, through Java, They had mentioned
about back references. They advice to reduce the usage of "page 1 of
20" formats because, before they completly generate the PDF, they
first "print" all the pages and then go ahead and fill in all the
backreferences to the page numbers. This way, it is very slow and
sometimes very memory hungry. I don't think it is going to be simple
for a HTML report. ( I din say it is impossible, but am short of ideas
at this moment).

From skarra at gmail.com  Mon May 28 13:33:56 2007
From: skarra at gmail.com (Sriram Karra)
Date: Mon May 28 13:34:05 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Structure copy behaviour
In-Reply-To: <e001dc80705232352u21f8fd63ydd548296fee81c51@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1179984180.8607.24.camel@sujith-lnx>
	<e001dc80705232352u21f8fd63ydd548296fee81c51@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <3f986c690705280103y5ed20a87nf8bfae60449fe656@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/24/07, Vaidhy <vaidhy@loonys.net> wrote:

> >From what I remember, gcc always does a shallow copy (case 1) and as an
> optimization, it implements copy-on-write.
> 
> Basically, assigning a[0] = t will have t and a[0] pointed to the same
> location till you change either t or a[0]. 

Sorry to put it so bluntly - but this is total nonsense. Where did pointers come in? 

Structure assignment is very much a part of the C standard at least since c99. In the "Constraints" part of the section titled "Simple Assignment" (Section 6.5.16.1) , we are told it is valid to have "the left operand has a qualified or unqualified version of a structure or union type compatible with the type of the right". The sematics of the copy is as you would expect it - a field to field copy; padding bits if any need not be copied.

-Karra

From m.sujith at gmail.com  Mon May 28 13:56:20 2007
From: m.sujith at gmail.com (Sujith Manoharan)
Date: Mon May 28 13:56:33 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Structure copy behaviour
In-Reply-To: <3f986c690705280107rcd680bfm3953d9bf8af87237@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1179984180.8607.24.camel@sujith-lnx>

	<1180071012.24560.15.camel@sujith-lnx>
	<def372aa0705250243i4606a05ci6e7154ce5626dd3@mail.gmail.com>
	<1180086643.8210.4.camel@sujith-lnx>
	<3f986c690705280107rcd680bfm3953d9bf8af87237@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1180340780.5988.26.camel@sujith-lnx>

On Mon, 2007-05-28 at 13:37 +0530, Sriram Karra wrote:
> I am curious to know why you are bothered that gcc generates a
> memcpy... It can, of course, be a matter of academic interest. However
> I do not understad what code you had to change, and why?
> 
> A structure assignment is .. a structure copy. It has well defined
> syntax and semantics defined by the C standard. I think I am missing
> something here. ?

To circumvent different calling conventions.
The caller and the callee have to agree on how they are going to pass
parameters between them.
The 'memcpy' definition can be part of a library that may have been
compiled with a different calling convention.

My code change was pretty simple, replace structure copies with calls to
a wrapper function over memcpy - but, with my preferred calling
convention enforced.

regards,
Sujith

From vaidhy at loonys.net  Mon May 28 14:00:47 2007
From: vaidhy at loonys.net (Vaidhy)
Date: Mon May 28 14:00:58 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Structure copy behaviour
In-Reply-To: <3f986c690705280103y5ed20a87nf8bfae60449fe656@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1179984180.8607.24.camel@sujith-lnx>
	<e001dc80705232352u21f8fd63ydd548296fee81c51@mail.gmail.com>
	<3f986c690705280103y5ed20a87nf8bfae60449fe656@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <e001dc80705280130q42e8d3earf47eb742ec15616f@mail.gmail.com>

C standards as most others only define the functionality, not the
implementation details. GCC and most other modern compilers implement
copy-on-write optimization since a lot of times, the values are copied to be
read, not changed.  And if you have to ask the question where the pointers
come in, I am left wondering if you are being illogical or sarcastic.

Please take a look at what copy-on-write(COW) means..
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copy_on_write

I do know for a fact that G++ strings are COW optimized and it is my guess
that structs are also optimized using COW.  I do not see how COW
optimization will break the standard since the compatibility checking will
be at compile time, while COW is at runtime.

A field by field copy is called a shallow copy. If one of the field values
is a pointer, the pointer will be copied as it is.  It is not always as you
expect it. I have been tripped enough times by not having a explicit copy
constructor in C++.

Regards,
Vaidhy

PS: If you think COW optimizations are not done in gcc for struct copy, it
is very easy to say so...

On 5/28/07, Sriram Karra <skarra@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> On 5/24/07, Vaidhy <vaidhy@loonys.net> wrote:
>
> > >From what I remember, gcc always does a shallow copy (case 1) and as an
> > optimization, it implements copy-on-write.
> >
> > Basically, assigning a[0] = t will have t and a[0] pointed to the same
> > location till you change either t or a[0].
>
> Sorry to put it so bluntly - but this is total nonsense. Where did
> pointers come in?
>
> Structure assignment is very much a part of the C standard at least since
> c99. In the "Constraints" part of the section titled "Simple Assignment"
> (Section 6.5.16.1) , we are told it is valid to have "the left operand has
> a qualified or unqualified version of a structure or union type compatible
> with the type of the right". The sematics of the copy is as you would expect
> it - a field to field copy; padding bits if any need not be copied.
>
> -Karra

On 5/28/07, Shuveb Hussain <shuveb@gmail.com> wrote:
>
>
> Interesting what marketting can do. All current Intel processors
> including the Celeron D, Pentium 4, Pentium D and C2D *are* 64 bit
> processors.


   Not all Netburst CPUs (Celeron D, Pentium 4 and Pentium D) are
64-bit enabled. Williamettes/Northwoods/Prescotts are 32-bit only,
while Smithfields and Cedar Mills are 32/64-bit. The general rule
is that all S478 P4s are 32-bit, while some LGA775 P4s are 64-bit
capable.

Use this URL to see what features your CPU supports:
> http://compare.intel.com
>
> If you got a recent P4, just pop in a 64 bit distro live/install CD
> and it should just work. Just that you didn't know it was 64 bit.
>

  The EM64T-enabled P4s will support x86-64 distros, but they
run inefficiently wrt to power consumption compared to a Core
2 Duo. Most dual-core P4s consume over 100W, while most
Allandales consume a maximum of 65W, and some idle as
low as 12W, depending on the ACPI C-state.

http://www.theinq.com/default.aspx?article=36446

This is for the 65nm C2Ds, BTW. The 45nm Penryn C2Ds due
in about 6 months improve on this figure of merit by a further
30-40%.

  As a rough thumb rule, if you're running 24/7/365, 1W of power
consumption costs about $1 yearly, including airconditioning
costs, etc. Dropping from 130W of the Pentium D to 65W of
the Allandale is a pretty big deal - it's about Rs.2.5k per node
per year in cost-cutting (consider the savings for Google with
~450,000 nodes in the Googleplex presently). The savings are
more with ACPI enabled and configured correctly.

-Siva

On 5/28/07, Saura Ramachandran <rsaura@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> On 5/26/07, Sivasankar Chander <siva.ilugc@gmail.com> wrote:
> >
> > IMHO, the best bang-for-the-buck presently is the Allandale Core 2 Duo
> > E4300 at 1.80GHz/800FSB/2MB L2 cache, at ~Rs.5k for the CPU.
>
> Please recommend a mother board avaialble in the local market, which
> runs Linux peacefully.


Any i965 or i975-based board should be fine. If you want to reuse DDR
RAM from an older board, any Via P4M890, P4M900, PT880 or PT890-
based motherboard should be fine.

  I'm partial to MSI, PowerColor, Foxconn and Intel-branded boards,
but there are a wide variety of reasonably good choices available in prices
ranging from Rs.2.5k to >Rs.15k. Just make sure that it supports your
choice of CPU, and combination of expansion slots (AGP/PCIe/PCI)
and you're good to go.

-Siva

Eventhough its extension is pdf, it is actually a ps file. (postscript).
To print in pdf, follow the steps,

Mostly, I face the problem in firefox in kubuntu.
I can not print to pdf directly from firefox.
Here is a tip to print to pdf in firefox itself.

File->print.
select "postscript/default" as printer.
select properties.
in "print command" give "kprinter"

now, give print.
It will ask you to print to pdf file.
give the pdf file name and again give print.

your pdf file is ready now.

From gshah at efrontier.com  Tue May 29 14:37:04 2007
From: gshah at efrontier.com (Gourav Shah)
Date: Tue May 29 14:38:04 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] installing packages using yast
In-Reply-To: <769402.92164.qm@web8911.mail.in.yahoo.com>
References: <769402.92164.qm@web8911.mail.in.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <465BED38.3070406@efrontier.com>

you need to add web repositories to yast to install packages over the 
internet. here are some pointers,
1. go to  Yast -> Software -> Installation sources
2. you'll find an option to add repositories.
here are the links where you could find some
http://en.opensuse.org/Package_Repositories
http://en.opensuse.org/Package_Repositories
3. enable web repositories and disable the local ones if you just want
to use the web based repositories only

hope this helps,

Thanks & Regards,
Gourav Shah

files. I think the problem is that DIVX decoders are not packaged with
ubuntu. If you install vlc media player from synaptic - it should
start working. installing vlc will install all additional required
libraries.

	<87ps4qtgs5.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>

On Fri, 25 May 2007 20:31:27 +0530, Anant Narayanan <anant@kix.in> said: 

> Manoj Srivastava wrote:
>> This is not an unlaudable goal.  I wonder, though, how prevalent that
>> view is amongst the worker bees of free software, as opposed to the
>> armchair intellectuals.  In my experience with people in free
>> software development, I have never heard the goal above stated as a
>> major motivator (and never heard it stated so succinctly).

> It is true that most of the *current* free software is a result of
> scratching your own itch. But the entire GNU project was founded with
> the exact goal of being a replacement for the then proprietary Unix -
> and what good is a replacement that isn't used by the masses?

        You do note that the GNU projects kernel is still barely self
 hosting; and the applications they managed to create never did reach
 critical mass.

        It took the scratch-the-itch folks to bring free software to
 where it is today.

> Over the course of time, free software developers; or the worker bees
> as you call them, have indeed forgotten the *original* motivation
> behind free software.

        You mean the motivation of the first few people who worked on
 the concept.  On the other hand, the people who scratch their itches
 are not the same as the people who came on earlier. And while I
 contributed work to X10/Emacs/Lucid Emacs way before there was was a
 Linux, I must confess I was still scratching my itch, even back then.

> A trend perhaps started by Linus - I doubt if he really knew the moral
> and social implications of releasing his kernel under the GPL. He did
> it simply because he really didn't care if the source was visible to
> the whole world, something that any university student his age would
> have done at the time.

        Lacking a crystal ball, I rarely make strong assertions about
 other people state of mind, motivations, or state of knowledge. I feel
 somewhat envious of your clarity into other peoples past
 motivations. Can we bowwor your crystal ball sometime?

> Let's not forget that the "armchair intellectuals" of today were the
> "worker bees" of the past, albeit, with different motivations.

        I kinda doubt that is the case with the majority of the
 bystanders. 

> - not only for the greater good of the society but also for the
> developers of the software itself. I quote from Frederick P. Brooke's
> "The Mythical Man-Month":

> "Why is programming fun? What delights may its practitioner expect as
> his reward? <snip> The pleasure of making things that are useful to
> others. Deep within, we want others to use our work and to find it
> helpful. In this respect the programming system is not essentially
> different from a child's first clay pencil holder `for daddy's
> office`"

        I think the world, and creating software, has changed a bit
 since IBM in the 60's.

> I don't question the motivation of free software developers. However,
> I oppose the view that free software developers shouldn't care about
> whether their software is used by the masses or not.

        Why should I, or any other software developer, care about what
 you think my view should be?

> As long as there are people ready to do the pushing voluntarily, the
> problem is solved.  The worker bees keep developing software for
> whatever reason - while others spend time in convincing the masses to
> use that software. The free software community is not only made of
> developers who can code but also of people who are interested in
> spreading its message.

        I have alway encouraged people to run with the marketing idea,
 if that floats their boat.  Whining about other people not running and
 performing according to their expectations is what I have been stating
 is something that does no one any good.

> Of course, a small percentage of free software developers *can* afford
> to keep "free society" as their major motivator: a vast majority of
> university professors and students - who have nothing to lose. And of
> course, people who are paid to write free software.

        If you think that someone on the publish or perish track has
 copious free time, you have not been in academe in a competitive
 university for long.

> The essence here is not to forget the original goal of free software
> development - and one of the objectives is to get the masses to use
> it.  The OLPC project is an exemplary example.

        It is the goal of the original developers.  The current crop has
 different motivators.  Just like you do not want to run your life based
 on my motivators, I have no desire to run mine based on other people's
 goals.

        manoj

> support Linux (for its lack of market share).

        I vote with my wallet.  So far, I think I am content with the
 trend in hardware support I am seeing; and see no reason to do things
 that I don't find fun, or to sacrifice on the altar of the god of
 marketing.

>> But you can't expect other people to work according to your belief
>> sets.

> So you cannot hold onto the view that hardware manufacturers who don't
> support Linux must be boycotted right? I am not even getting to the
> part where this (boycotting) isn't enforcing your opinion on hardware
> manufacturers or not.

        I do not buy hardware I can't use.  I do not resume to tell
 other people what to do. If the hadware vendor wants my custom, and the
 custom of the companies whose buying decisions I influence; they''l
 provide Linux support.  Those that do not lose my patronage.

        Such is how the free market works.


> You cannot decide to remain a niche player while simultaneously
> expecting hardware manufactures to cater to you as if you are a mass
> market player.

        I am not trying to coerce anyone else to do anything they do not
 want.  I am happy with my current level of hardware support --heck, I
 was happy with it 10 years ago.

> I haven't received any response from you on this except the same tired
> arguments which I have already addressed.

        And you with the same whining as usual.

        If you want marketing to be done, get off your butt and do
 something about it. Stop haranguing other people who have no interest
 in doing so.

        manoj
-- 
PARDON me, am I speaking ENGLISH?
Manoj Srivastava <srivasta@acm.org> <http://www.golden-gryphon.com/>

From snwasim at midascomm.com  Thu May 31 11:12:14 2007
From: snwasim at midascomm.com (Wasim Akram S.N.)
Date: Thu May 31 11:10:15 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] installing openoffice
In-Reply-To: <436759.48383.qm@web8909.mail.in.yahoo.com>
References: <436759.48383.qm@web8909.mail.in.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <465E6036.8000903@midascomm.com>

satheesh kumar wrote:
> hi,
> i am using rhel4.
> it had openoffice-1.1
> i downloaded openoffice.org-2.2 and tried to update my openoffice as 
> # rpm -Uvh openoffice.org-*
> in the directory where i had openoffice.org-2.2 RPMS.
> but i could use only ooffice1.1
> then i removed ooffice as,
> #rpm -e openoffice.org
>
> now when am trying to install ooffice2.2 i am unable to install it as it says that the packages have been installed already. 
>
> i cant find the binary ooffice too in my system. How to make my ooffice2.2 to work??
>   

You can find your openoffice installation on

/opt/openoffice.org2.2/

directory.

configure gnome or kde to use openoffice to open the files with 
appropriate extension.

for eg.

use scalc  program in /opt/openoffice.org2.2/program/ directory  to open 
files with .xls extension

Thanks & Regards,
Wasim Akram S.N.
